        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842"><meta property="og:title" content="Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c00842.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Presodiation has shown great promise in compensating sodium storage losses. In the absence of a mechanistic understanding of how presodiation affects the surface of an electrode material, packaging optimization is restricted. Focusing on interfaces, we illustrate the working principle of presodiation in virtue of short-circuiting internal circuits. The presodiated carbon nanotubes (PS-CNTs) provide a thin, denser, and more robust solid electrolyte interfacial layer, enabling a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), high power density, and cycling stability with the merits of uniformly distributed NaF. As a result, our assembled sodium-ion battery (SIB) full cell with PS-CNT has an ICE of 91.6% and an energy density of 226 Wh kg–1, which was superior to the pristine CNT control electrode (ICE of 42.9% and energy density of 163 Wh kg–1). The gained insights can be practically applied to directly promote the commercial uses of carbon-based materials in sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c00842.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187f040d47075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f040d47075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '55aea801-534e-409d-b298-55b33680cc2b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f040d47075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187f040d47075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Presodiation Architected Robust Surface Enables Packaging Optimal Performance of Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">6</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0006.gif" alt="" id="_i2" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Presodiation has shown great promise in compensating sodium storage losses. In the absence of a mechanistic understanding of how presodiation affects the surface of an electrode material, packaging optimization is restricted. Focusing on interfaces, we illustrate the working principle of presodiation in virtue of short-circuiting internal circuits. The presodiated carbon nanotubes (PS-CNTs) provide a thin, denser, and more robust solid electrolyte interfacial layer, enabling a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), high power density, and cycling stability with the merits of uniformly distributed NaF. As a result, our assembled sodium-ion battery (SIB) full cell with PS-CNT has an ICE of 91.6% and an energy density of 226 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, which was superior to the pristine CNT control electrode (ICE of 42.9% and energy density of 163 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). The gained insights can be practically applied to directly promote the commercial uses of carbon-based materials in sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7180%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMeiqi%2BLiu%252C%2BWenwen%2BLi%252C%2BFuxi%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D24%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c00842%26title%3DPresodiation%2BArchitected%2BRobust%2BSurface%2BEnables%2BPackaging%2BOptimal%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7187%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291646&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanotubes"> Carbon nanotubes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry"> Surface chemistry </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Presodiation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Presodiation">Presodiation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Solid+Electrolyte+Interface+Layer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Solid%20electrolyte%20interface%20layer">Solid electrolyte interface layer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">Initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanotube&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Carbon%20nanotube">Carbon nanotube</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">The sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are complementary to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for grid energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> A low-cost, high-performance electrode material is essential for SIBs’ success. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> For the randomly distributed crystallites within a hard carbon (HC), more charge can be stored in a smaller volume, increasing the energy density and thereof expanding the battery working range. However, it remains a long way from being industrialized scale nowadays. Hard carbon, despite its high capacity, may result in serious side reactions and irreversible sodium insertion due to its abundance of defects and large specific surface area. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> The HC anode has poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which negatively affects the capacity and cycle stability of a full cell. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> The ICE of hard carbon can be improved by several approaches. A temperature and warming rate adjustment during synthesis is used to tailor HC’s microstructure, such as increasing graphitization and reducing defect sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> Or increasing closed pores in the material or modulating defects enables also improving sodium storage properties of HC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a></p><p>Considering that prelithiation/presodiation has been proposed to compensate for cation losses in the electrode before cycling, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> three categories are divided with chemical, electrochemical and direct contact methods. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Unfortunately, the problem of reagent residues is unavoidable in chemically reactive presodiation, and in addition the process complexity greatly reduces the feasibility of electrochemical presodiation in large-scale production, putting a serious obstacle to its commercialization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Sodium metal is an efficient and direct presodiation reagent that has a high theoretical specific capacity (1166 mAh/g) and produces no byproducts after presodiation. The electrode and sodium foil/sodium power can be directly rolled contact for presodiation, following the process adopted by LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> Although presodiation of carbon-based anodes unquestionably increases ICE, it is yet unclear how presodiation affects the long-term cycling stability of entire cells. Since almost all reports have focused on performance enhancement rather than clarifying the presodiation mechanism, presodiation degree has not been considered as a necessary parameter. The commercialization of sodium-ion batteries will be not promoted until a thorough characterization and full analysis of the presodiation products.</p><p>Herein we use a short-circuit method to pre-embed some Na<sup>+</sup> into the hard carbon nanotubes (CNTs). During the presodiation process, a thinner, denser and NaF-rich artificial solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film is preformed on the surface of CNTs. It not only enhances the interfacial ion transport but also reduces the irreversible loss of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus improving the ICE. Time of flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) reveals that sodium ions are not only present on the surface of CNTs, but also within its interior, which will serve as an additional reservoir for sodium ions. The full cell assembled with presodiated CNT (PS-CNT) has an ICE of 91.6% and an energy density of 226 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, while the sodium-ion capacitor assembled with it lasts for 6000 cycles. Thus, optimizing the presodiation degree of a material is an easy way to stabilize the cycle life of carbon-based batteries and increase their capacities.</p><p>High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of the prepared carbon nanotubes (CNTs) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, showing a disorderedly crystallites involving amounts of turbostratic nanodomains and nanogaps. Selected area electron diffraction (SAED, inset) indicates amorphous properties (i.e., disordered crystal structure) that are different from those of conventional graphite. X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2–3</a>) further confirm this issue. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption showed that CNT is mesoporous in nature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Based on its structural features, the prepared CNTs belong to hard carbon.</p><p>The presodiation process is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a, where a little amount of electrolyte is used to wet the anode and afford direct contact with the sodium metal foil. The Na metal electrode is weighted during presodiation to ensure a tight contact. A potential difference exists between the sodium metal layer and the active material, i.e., what drives electron flow. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> Afterward, Na<sup>+</sup> is transported into the electrode for charge neutralization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> It is possible to compensate for the initial loss of sodium by inserting sodium metal into the active material and bringing it into contact with the electrode. Due to the fact that it is similar to a short circuit in a primary cell, presodiation occurs quickly and completely. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a></p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Electrochemical performance of CNT half-cells with different degrees of sodiation. (a) Schematic diagram of the presodiation process. (b) Charge/discharge curves for different presodiation times and (c) Comparison of charge/discharge specific capacity and open-circuit voltage in the first cycle. (d) Time-dependent study of PS-CNT. The presodium capacity was calculated as 323.36 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (initial sodium capacity). (e) Comparison of cycling performance at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Adjusting the reaction time enables modulating the degree of presodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> To optimize the presodiation, we compared charging/discharging behavior and ICE values at various presodiation times. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, c, the pristine CNT anode exhibited a high initial discharge (sodiation) capacity (323.36 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), an irreversible capacity loss of 114.75 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and an ICE was only 64.5%. Discharge capacity gradually reduces as the presodiation time increases, linked to the decreased amount of Na<sup>+</sup> embedded in the anode. Pristine and presodiated CNT samples displayed similar voltage profiles during the sodium removal, suggesting that presodiation has no effect on CNT behaviors. The ICE of CNT-5, CNT-30, CNT-60, and CNT-120 were 460.4%, 607.3%, 613.5%, and 694.6%, respectively. It is generally believed that the presodiation is completed when the ICE reaches 100%, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref26" aria-label="reference 21 and 26">(21,26)</a> but herein it was found that CNT had a higher specific capacity when the ICE was 613.5% at 60 min of presodiation. In this case, the Coulombic efficiency should not be the basis for completing the presodiation, but rather, the embedded sodium ions and the utilization rate should be considered.</p><p>We systematically measured the amount of sodium preloaded into CNTs in response to presodiation time (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d). The presodiation capacity was calculated as first cycle charging capacity - first cycle discharging capacity, and the percentage of sodiation was calculated from (presodiation capacity)/(total capacity). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Na<sup>+</sup> embedding was more active within the first 60 min, as measured by the positive correlation between the desodiation capacity and presodiation time. After 60 min, CNT sodiation reached 73.2%. Overpresodiation (e.g., 120 min) deteriorated the desodiation capacity, which was related to ion transport limitations. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique showed lower diffusion coefficients after 120 min of presodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>).</p><p>The effect of presodiation time on cycling stability was investigated using half-cells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). They showed flat cycling curves, suggesting that presodiation does not affect anode’s cycle stability, whereas the sample with 60 min of presodiation showed an increased capacity. As a consequence, we chose a PS-CNT to have lasted 60 min. Through scanning electron microscopy (SEM), the morphology and structure of the PS-CNT electrodes were examined before and after the presodiation treatment. It showed that the morphology remained essentially the same, but the diameters changed. A statistical comparison was made between the diameters of the CNTs prior to and after presodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). In response to presodiation, the average diameter of CNTs changed from ∼100 to ∼130, ∼170, and ∼200 nm, indicating the sodiation and volume expansion. Solid electrolyte interfaces can also increase the diameter of CNTs.</p><p>With the increase in presodiation time, the (002) peak shifted left (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a-b), proving the embedding of Na<sup>+</sup>, consistent with the layer spacing increase, also confirmed by Raman and FTIR of PS-CNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7–8</a>). Although the time dependence of presodiation was established in the previous section (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), the precise amount of Na<sup>+</sup> embedded in the anode material remains unclear. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) was used to measure sodium content in CNTs with different presodiation times, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c. As expected, the Na content increased nonlinearly from 0 to 10% with the increasing presodiation time. However, after 30 min, the embedding of sodium ions was leveled off as the time continued to increase and finally reached saturation. A presodiation capacity of 277.26 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved after 60 min of presodiation, and sodium loading in the CNT active material was ∼10 at%. In practice, this relationship is used to guide how much sodium is consumed in order to avoid sodium loss. Simulation of sodium content versus pretreatment time is shown in the red curve.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Control of presodiation degrees. (a) X-ray Diffraction (XRD) images of CNT after 0–60 min of presodiation. (b) A localized magnified XRD pattern for a. (c) Correlation between presodiation time and the Na content preloaded into CNT. The simulated red lines are only for guidance. Correlative SEM images (d-f) and TOF-SIMS (g-i), showing Na<sup>+</sup> distribution imaging of CNT-5, CNT-30, and CNT-60, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The TOF-SIMS analysis of sodium content on CNT surfaces was conducted (red color for Na<sup>+</sup>), and the color deepening indicates that the sodium content increases with the increase of presodiation time (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g-i), which is consistent with the above results. According to the curve of sodium content with depth (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), the distribution of sodium is more homogeneous at 60 min. It also shows that Na<sup>+</sup> distribution is embedded in the anode material rather than just on the surface, added to sodium ion sources. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d-f shows the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images corresponding to TOF-SIMS. Embedding of Na-ions expands the layer spacing and enhances the electrochemical performance of CNTs.</p><p>The electrochemical performance of Na//pristine CNT and Na//PS-CNT were compared to reveal the effects of presodiation on CNT anodes. The first CV curve of PS-CNT does not show a reduction peak for SEI film formation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a), while only one reduction peak appears around 0.07 V, indicating that SEI film was preformed on PS-CNT surface. Additionally, the CV peaks on the anode seemed to have been narrower and remained almost unchanged in the subsequent cycles, suggesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process had become more and faster following the first cycle. A rapidly decreasing discharge curve was observed due to SEI formation on the PS-CNT electrode at 1.5–1 V without a tilted shoulder (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). A portion of the sodium formed a SEI film during the first discharge, explaining the ICE of the pristine CNT of 64.51%. The other portion of the irreversible capacity was caused by the inability to remove Na<sup>+</sup> embedded in the micropores and graphite interlayers, which prevented Na<sup>+</sup> from being dislodged. After the presodiation, the discharge specific capacity increased from 207.43 to 254.43 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the charge specific capacity also increased from 197.83 to 254.87 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the second cycle. At different current densities ranging, both the CNT and PS-CNT anodes were evaluated for their capacity. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, the performance of PS-CNT was still slightly higher than that of untreated CNT even at high currents. Furthermore, both the CNT and PS-CNT electrodes displayed good cycling performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), which was probably the result of their hollow structure, which allowed mechanical stresses induced by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and removal to be released. The capacity retention of the pristine CNT and PS-CNT after 300 cycles was 80.87% and 81.4%, respectively (except for the first cycle), indicating that the presodiation was not harmful to the cyclability of the CNT anode.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Comparison of electrochemical properties. (a) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Pristine CNT as well as PS-CNT at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Comparison of charge/discharge curves. (c) Comparison of rate performance. (d) Comparison of cycling stability at 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Schematic diagram of the full battery; (f) and (g) Charge/discharge curves of Pristine CNT//NVP as well as PS-CNT//NVP full batteries. (h) Full battery cycling performance comparison.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>There is a lot of Na-ions present in the half cell due to the sodium foil serving as the counter electrode, so the effect of the presodiation treatment on the performance of the half-cell is not obvious. Presodiation improves the performance of a full cell more visibly since the cathode material contains fewer Na<sup>+</sup>. PS-CNTs assembled into a full cell with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode were compared with the pristine CNT. The morphology and electrochemical properties of NVP can be found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11–S14</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f shows the voltage profiles of the first two cycles of pristine CNT//NVP, and at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the full cell exhibits an ICE of 42.92% as well as charging/discharging capacities of 173.38 and 74.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼163 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). In contrast, the PS-CNT//NVP exhibited a high ICE of 91.6% and a charging/discharging capacity of more than 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (∼226 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) and an operating voltage that exceeded that of the pristine CNT//NVP. The operating voltage of the PS-CNT//NVP is ∼3.3 V, whereas the average operating voltage of CNT//NVP is ∼2.5 V. Counting from the second cycle, PS-CNT//NVP has 83% capacity retention after 60 cycles, while the pristine-CNT//NVP has only 39.4%. Preloaded sodium for PS-CNT can be present in the anode as a sodium stock after compensating for the loss of sodium during the first cycle. As the sodium source is continuously replenished during cycling, the cycle stability of the full battery will be improved by presodiation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h).</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Nanostructure of the SEI on the PS-CNT visualized by TEM. (a, c-d, f-g, j, l-o) PS-CNT. (b, h-i, k) CNT-first. (e) Pristine CNT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>A closed contact interface between the sodium source and anode allows electron transport, sodium metal ionization, and anodic sodiation to occur. Therefore, the evolution of the interface, consisting of sodium metal dissolution and SEI formation, is bound to have a combined effect on the reaction kinetics, especially on electron transport. TEM and XPS characterizations on the electrode after presodiation and first discharge (CNT-first) in order to investigate its interfacial composition. PS-CNT and CNT-first still retain the intrinsic tubular structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b). The layer spacing of CNT after presodiation increased (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c-d), again verifying the insertion of Na-ions. To prevent any possible errors, we performed extensive statistics on the variation of layer spacing (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15–16</a>). The spontaneous SEI formation was observed on the CNT electrode following short-circuit pretreatment (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e-i), whose thickness is ∼5–7 nm, shorter than the SEI formed on the surface after the first discharge (∼11 nm for CNT-first). Both PS-CNT and CNT-first SEI membranes contained inorganic phases, which can be categorized as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> based on the distinctive lattice spacing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j-o). Such result is consistent with the previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> These inorganic particles have a lot of mosaic structural grain boundaries and are dispersed in irregular forms over an amorphous organic substrate. This can facilitate the transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> at the interface, which can speed up reaction kinetics.</p><p>The chemical composition of the SEI layer was characterized at the electrodes by depth profiling XPS analysis and the surface chemistry of the two electrodes was very similar in terms of peak positions. The peaks in the 286.1 and 289.6 eV spectra are attributed to the C–O and C = O/Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> The increase in O–C = O/C = O concentration at the cycled electrode compared to the pristine electrode was due to the decomposition of Na<sup>–</sup>(DME)<sup>+</sup><sub><i>x</i></sub> and the formation of an SEI layer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). In the O 1s spectra, organic species were observed in both SEI layers. Peaks at 531.4 and 532.8 eV were assigned to the R–O-Na bonds of DME and to the carbonate groups produced by the decomposition of the come. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> In the XPS of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a-h, the two peaks at 683.8 and 686.9 eV in the F spectra of PS-CNT and CNT belong to NaF and organic P–F species, respectively, originated from the decomposition and the residue of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. NaF was found to be dominant and uniformly distributed in the SEI of PS-CNT, indicating its inorganic-rich nature (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). NaF is known to have high mechanical stability and high interfacial energy to inhibit the growth of Na dendrites, which can accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> migration in SEI. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Na was not present in the pristine CNT samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>), whereas the presence of Na–F/Na-O was found in PS-CNT and CNT-first (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d,h), suggesting the in situ generation of SEI during the presodiation process, which is similar to the process of generating SEI in electrochemical reactions.</p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/medium/nl4c00842_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XPS analysis of the SEI layer. (a,e) C 1s (b,f) O 1s (c,g) F 1s (d,h) Na 1s XPS spectra of SEI formed by PS-CNT and CNT-first for 0 and 10 s sputtering times. Surface element content variation with etching depth for PS-CNT (i) and (j) CNT-first. (k) Comparison of surface element content before etching. (l) Comparison of peak areas of inorganic constituents (O–C = O, CO<sub class="stack">3</sub><sup class="stack">2–</sup>, Na–F) in the SEI film formed by PS-CNT and CNT-first.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/asset/images/large/nl4c00842_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>In the first few parts of the etching, the content of fluorine (F) and oxygen (O) decreases, while carbon and sodium increase. This is because inorganic species including Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF are present in the outer layer of SEI, while sodium is not only involved in the SEI composition, but also embedded in the material. Thus, the content increases, consistent with the results of the flight secondary mass spectrometry. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>k-l shows the elemental composition of SEI film before etching as well as the comparison of O–C = O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and NaF peak areas of inorganic components in it. The inorganic component of SEI has a higher Na conductivity than its organic counterpart, which is the reason for the improved performance of PS-CNT anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref34" aria-label="reference 28 and 34">(28,34)</a></p><p>Electrode impedance spectroscopy was performed with both the presodiation and unpresodiation CNTs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, the impedance values obtained from a Nyquist plot are summarized using an equivalent circuit model. It can be seen that the PS-CNT has a lower contact resistance (3.036 Ω) and a lower charge transfer resistance (27.94 Ω), and the lower value of Rs suggests that the Na transfer through the SEI is faster, which is responsible for the improved rate performance of the PS-CNT.</p><p>To investigate the generalizability of this presodiation method, Sodium ion capacitors (SICs) were assembled with CNT and activated carbon (AC, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20–21</a>). The SIC without sodium replenishment has a shorter sustainability discharge time, whereas after pre-embedding with Na<sup>+</sup>, the discharge time is significantly longer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22–25</a>). By replenishing sodium in advance, the Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte is retained for the subsequent charge and discharge since it compensates for the Na<sup>+</sup> consumed during SEI film generation. The SIC has an energy density of 57.3 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> at 120 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, which integrates high energy density and power density compared to other devices. It can light up the logo of JLU consisting of 60 LED bulbs, and after 6000 ultralong cycles, the SIC showed excellent capacity retention of ∼79% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>). In addition, we used commercial HC (CHC) as the base material and presodiated it in the same way, and similar results were obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26–29</a>). Clearly, the advantages of the presodiation strategy show great potential for practical applications.</p><p class="last">As a result of eliminating the irreversible capacity of the CNT anode after presodiation, all sodium ions supplied by the cathode can be fully utilized, increasing the energy density of the cell (from 163 to 226 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) with excellent cycle stability. NaF-rich SEI forms on the surface of the PS-CNT formed from sodium pre-embedded on its surface, promoting reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and removal and enhancing interfacial reaction kinetics. It improved the initial Coulombic efficiency of the full cell of PS-CNT//NVP from 42.92% to 91.6%, and the PS-CNT//AC had a capacity retention of 79% over 6000 cycles. This presodiation process is extremely gentle, easy to use, and improves the ICE of carbon-based materials. This will greatly facilitate the development of SIBs and SICs for commercial use.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Material synthesis of CNT, presodiation process, characterization and additional electrochemical testing results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842/suppl_file/nl4c00842_si_001.pdf">nl4c00842_si_001.pdf (2.03 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c00842" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202070134"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hirsh, H.                 10.1007/s41918-022-00174-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2023%26pages%3D5%26journal%3DElectrochem.%2BEnergy%2BRev.%26issue%3D1%26author%3DT.%2BBashir%26author%3DS.%2BZhou%26author%3DS.%2BYang%26author%3DS.%2BA.%2BIsmail%26author%3DT.%2BAli%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BZhao%26author%3DL.%2BGao%26title%3DProgress%2Bin%2B3D-MXene%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BLithium%252FSodium%252FPotassium%252FMagnesium%252FZinc%252FAluminum-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs41918-022-00174-2&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs41918-022-00174-2&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Progress in 3D-MXene Electrodes for Lithium/Sodium/Potassium/Magnesium/Zinc/Aluminum-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bashir, Tariq; Zhou, Shaowen; Yang, Shiqi; Ismail, Sara Adeeba; Ali, Tariq; Wang, Hao; Zhao, Jianqing; Gao, Lijun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EERLAM</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8136</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: MXenes have attracted increasing attention because of their rich surface functional groups, high elec. cond., and outstanding dispersibility in many solvents, and have demonstrated competitive efficiency in energy storage and conversion applications.  However, the restacking nature of MXene nanosheets like other two-dimensional (2D) materials through van der Waals forces results in sluggish ionic kinetics, restricted no. of active sites, and ultimate deterioration of MXene material/device performance.  The strategy of raising 2D MXenes into three-dimensional (3D) structures has been considered an efficient way for reducing restacking, providing greater porosity, higher surface area, and shorter distances for mass transport of ions, surpassing std. one-dimensional (1D) and 2D structures.  In multivalent ion batteries, the pos. multivalent ions combine with two or more electrons at the same time, so their capacities are two or three times that of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) under the same conditions, e.g., a magnesium ion battery has a high theor. specific capacity of 2 205 mAh g-1 and a high volumetric capacity of 3 833 mAh cm-3.  In this review, we summarize the most recent strategies for fabricating 3D MXene architectures, such as assembly, template, 3D printing, electrospinning, aerogel, and gas foaming methods.  Special consideration has been given to the applications of highly porous 3D MXenes in energy storage devices beyond LIBs, such as sodium ion batteries (SIBs), potassium ion batteries (KIBs), magnesium ion batteries (MIBs), zinc ion batteries (ZIBs), and aluminum ion batteries (AIBs).  Finally, the authors provide a summary of the future opportunities and challenges for the construction of 3D MXenes and MXene-based electrodes for applications beyond LIBs.  Graphic Abstr.: [graphic not available: see fulltext].</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201801879"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Progress and Challenges</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">133</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">144</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/cssc.201801879</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D133-144%26journal%3DChemSusChem%26issue%3D1%26author%3DB.%2BXiao%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26author%3DX.%2BLi%26title%3DHard%2BCarbon%2Bas%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%253A%2BProgress%2Band%2BChallenges%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fcssc.201801879&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c00842&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801879&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Progress and Challenges</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Biwei; Rojo, Teofilo; Li, Xiaolin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Hard C (HC) is the state-of-the-art anode material for Na-ion batteries due to its excellent overall performance, wide availability, and relatively low cost.  Recently, tremendous effort has been invested to elucidate the sodium storage mechanism in HC, and to explore synthetic approaches that can enhance the performance and lower the cost.  However, disagreements remain in the field, particularly on the fundamental questions of ion transfer and storage and the ideal HC structure for high performance.  This paper provides an anal. and summary of the theor. limitations of HC, discrepancies in the storage mechanism, and methods to improve the performance.  Future research on developing ideal structured HCs, advanced electrolytes, and optimized electrolyte-electrode interphases are proposed on the basis of recent progress.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202302277"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosting the Development of Hard Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Strategies to Optimize the Initial Coulombic Efficiency</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting the Development of Hard Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Strategies to Optimize the Initial Coulombic Efficiency</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yunrui; Wu, Chun; He, Xiang-Xi; Zhao, Jiahua; Yang, Zhuo; Li, Lin; Wu, Xingqiao; Li, Li; Chou, Shu-Lei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2024</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Given the merits of affordable cost, superior low-temp. performance, and advanced safe properties, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have exhibited great development potential in large scale energy storage applications.  Among various emerging carbonaceous anode materials applied for SIBs, hard carbon (HC) has recently gained significant attention regarding their relatively low cost, wide availability, and optimal overall performance.  However, the insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC is the main bottlenecks, which is inevitably hindering their further com. applications.  Herein, an in-depth holistic exposition about the reasons causing the unsatisfied ICE and the recent advances on effective improvement strategies are comprehensively summarized in this review, which have been divided into two aspects including the intrinsic property (degree of graphitization, pore structure, defect, et al.) and the extrinsic factor (electrolyte, electrode materials, et al.).  In addn., future prospects and perspectives on HC to enable practical application in SIBs are also briefly outlined.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1007/s41918-022-00178-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of Carbonaceous Materials for Li/Na-Ion Batteries: Origins, Solutions, and Perspectives</span>. <i>Electrochem.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Carbon Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Tengfei; Li, Chen; Wang, Fan; Noori, Abolhassan; Mousavi, Mir F.; Xia, Xinhui; Zhang, Yongqi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Record</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
